<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg/220px-Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg/330px-Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg/440px-Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="441" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basor_Dalit_caste.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Basor" title="Basor">Basor</a></i> weaving bamboo baskets in a 1916 book. </tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template talk:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Discrimination_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Caste</b> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social stratification</a> characterized by <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">endogamy</a>, hereditary transmission of a style of life which often includes an occupation, ritual status in a hierarchy, and customary social interaction and exclusion based on cultural notions of purity and pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScottMarshall200566_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScottMarshall200566-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinthrop199127–30_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinthrop199127–30-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>   Its paradigmatic  ethnographic example is the division of India's <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> society into rigid social groups, with roots in India's ancient history and persisting to the present time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBéteille200266_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBéteille200266-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  However, the economic significance of the <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_India" title="Caste system in India">caste system in India</a> has been declining as a result of urbanization and affirmative action programs. A subject of much scholarship by sociologists and anthropologists, the Hindu caste system is sometimes used as an analogical basis for the study of caste-like social divisions existing outside Hinduism and India. The term "caste" is also applied to morphological groupings in female populations of ants and bees.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English word "caste" derives from the Spanish and Portuguese <i><a href="/wiki/Casta" title="Casta">casta</a></i>, which, according to the <a href="/wiki/John_Minsheu" title="John Minsheu">John Minsheu</a>'s Spanish dictionary (1569), means "race, lineage, tribe or breed".<sup id="cite_ref-oed-caste_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-caste-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>  When the Spanish colonized the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, they used the word to mean a "clan or lineage". It was, however, the Portuguese who first employed <i>casta</i> in the primary modern sense of the English word 'caste' when they applied it to the thousands of endogamous, hereditary Indian social groups they encountered upon their arrival in India in 1498.<sup id="cite_ref-oed-caste_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-caste-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pitt-rivers_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pitt-rivers-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  The use of the spelling "caste", with this latter meaning, is first attested in English in 1613.<sup id="cite_ref-oed-caste_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-caste-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Caste_system_in_India">Caste system in India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Caste system in India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_India" title="Caste system in India">Caste system in India</a></div>
<p>Modern India's caste system is based on the artificial superimposition of a four-fold theoretical classification called the <i><a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">Varna</a></i> on the natural social groupings called the <i><a href="/wiki/J%C4%81ti" title="Jāti">Jāti</a></i>. From 1901 onwards, for the purposes of the Decennial Census, the British classified all Jātis into one or the other of the <i><a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">Varna</a></i> categories as described in ancient texts. Herbert Hope Risley, the Census Commissioner, noted that "The principle suggested as a basis was that of classification by social precedence as recognized by native public opinion at the present day, and manifesting itself in the facts that particular castes are supposed to be the modern representatives of one or other of the castes of the theoretical Indian system."<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The system of <i>Varnas</i> propounded in ancient Hindu texts envisages the society divided into four classes: <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmins</a> (scholars and yajna priests), <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriyas</a> (rulers and warriors), <a href="/wiki/Vaishyas" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaishyas">Vaishyas</a> (farmers, merchants and artisans) and <a href="/wiki/Shudras" class="mw-redirect" title="Shudras">Shudras</a> (workmen/service providers). The texts do not mention any separate, untouchable category in <i><a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">Varna</a></i> classification. Scholars believe that the <i>Varnas</i> system was never truly operational in society and there is no evidence of it ever being a reality in Indian history. The practical division of the society had always been in terms of <i>Jātis</i> (birth groups), which are not based on any specific principle, but could vary from ethnic origins to occupations to geographic areas. The <i>Jātis</i> have been endogamous groups without any fixed hierarchy but subject to vague notions of rank articulated over time based on lifestyle and social, political or economic status. Many of India's major empires and dynasties like the Mauryas,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Shalivahanas,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Chalukyas,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Kakatiyas<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> among many others, were founded by people who would have been classified as Shudras, under the <i>Varnas</i> system. It is well established that by the 9th century, kings from all the four castes, including Brahmins and Vaishyas, had occupied the highest seat in the monarchical system in Hindu India, contrary to the Varna theory.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In many instances, as in Bengal, historically the kings and rulers had been called upon, when required, to mediate on the ranks of <i>Jātis</i>, which might number in thousands all over the subcontinent and vary by region. In practice, the <i>jātis</i> may or may not fit into the <i>Varna</i> classes and many prominent <i>Jatis</i>, for example the Jats and Yadavs, straddled two Varnas i.e. Kshatriyas and Vaishyas, and the <i>Varna</i> status of <i>Jātis</i> itself was subject to articulation over time.
</p><p>Starting with the British colonial Census of 1901 led by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hope_Risley" title="Herbert Hope Risley">Herbert Hope Risley</a>, all the <i>jātis</i> were grouped under the theoretical <i>varnas</i> categories.<sup id="cite_ref-dirks_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirks-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> According to political scientist <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Rudolph" title="Lloyd Rudolph">Lloyd Rudolph</a>, Risley believed that <i>varna</i>, however ancient, could be applied to all the modern castes found in India, and "[he] meant to identify and place several hundred million Indians within it."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In an effort to arrange various castes in order of precedence functional grouping was based less on the occupation that prevailed in each case in the present day than on that which was traditional with it, or which gave rise to its differentiation from the rest of the community. "This action virtually removed Indians from the progress of history and condemned them to an unchanging position and place in time. In one sense, it is rather ironic that the British, who continually accused the Indian people of having a static society, should then impose a construct that denied progress"<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>  The terms <i>varna</i> (conceptual classification based on occupation) and <i>jāti</i> (groups) are two distinct concepts: while <i>varna</i> is the idealised four-part division envisaged by the <a href="/wiki/Dvija" title="Dvija">Twice-Borns</a>, <i>jāti</i> (community) refers to the thousands of actual endogamous groups prevalent across the subcontinent. The classical authors scarcely speak of anything other than the <i>varnas</i>, as it provided a convenient shorthand; but a problem arises when even Indologists sometimes confuse the two.<sup id="cite_ref-Chicago_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chicago-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Thus, starting with the 1901 Census, Caste officially became India's essential institution, with an imprimatur from the British administrators, augmenting a discourse that had already dominated Indology. “Despite India's acquisition of formal political independence, it has still not regained the power to know its own past and present apart from that discourse”.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon independence from Britain, the Indian Constitution listed 1,108 castes across the country as Scheduled Castes in 1950, for positive discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The Untouchable communities are sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Scheduled_Castes" class="mw-redirect" title="Scheduled Castes">Scheduled Castes</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dalit" title="Dalit">Dalit</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Harijan" title="Harijan">Harijan</a></i> in contemporary literature.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 2001, Dalits were 16.2% of India's population.<sup id="cite_ref-censtat01_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censtat01-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Most of the 15 million bonded child workers are from the lowest castes.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Independent India has witnessed <a href="/wiki/Caste-related_violence_in_India" title="Caste-related violence in India">caste-related violence</a>. In 2005, government recorded approximately 110,000 cases of reported violent acts, including rape and murder, against Dalits.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> For 2012, the government recorded 651 murders, 3,855 injuries, 1,576 rapes, 490 kidnappings, and 214 cases of arson.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The socio-economic limitations of the caste system are reduced due to urbanization and <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a>. Nevertheless, the caste system still exists in <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">endogamy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Family_patrimony" title="Family patrimony">patrimony</a>, and thrives in the politics of democracy, where caste provides ready made constituencies to politicians. The globalization and economic opportunities from foreign businesses has influenced the growth of India's middle-class population. Some members of the Chhattisgarh Potter Caste Community (CPCC) are middle-class urban professionals and no longer potters unlike the remaining majority of traditional rural potter members. The co-existence of the middle-class and traditional members in the CPCC has created <a href="/wiki/Intersectionality" title="Intersectionality">intersectionality</a> between caste and class.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> There is persistence of caste in Indian politics. Caste associations have evolved into caste-based political parties. Political parties and the state perceive caste as an important factor for mobilization of people and policy development.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies by Bhatt and Beteille have shown changes in status, openness, mobility in the social aspects of Indian society. As a result of the modern social pressures on the country, India is experiencing a change in their social sphere dynamic as well as economically in the caste system.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> While arranged marriages are still the most common practice in India, the internet has provided a network for younger Indians to take control of their relationships through the use of dating apps. This remains isolated to informal terms, as marriage is not often achieved through the use of these apps.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Hypergamy is still a common practice in India and Hindu culture. Men are expected to marry within their caste, or one below, with no social repercussions. If a woman marries into a higher caste, then her children will take the status of their father. If she marries down, her family is reduced to the social status of their son in law. In this case, the women are bearers of the egalitarian principle of the marriage. There would be no benefit in marrying a higher caste if the terms of the marriage did not imply equality.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> However, men are systematically shielded from the negative implications of the agreement.
</p><p>Geographical factors also determine adherence to the caste system. Many Northern villages are more likely to participate in exogamous marriage, due to a lack of eligible suitors within the same caste. Women in North India have been found to be less likely to leave or divorce their husbands since they are of a relatively lower caste system, and have higher restrictions on their freedoms. On the other hand, Pahari women, of the northern mountains, have much more freedom to leave their husbands without stigma. This often leads to better husbandry as his actions are not protected by social expectations.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chiefly among the factors influencing the rise of exogamy is the rapid <a href="/wiki/Urbanisation_in_India" title="Urbanisation in India">urbanisation in India</a> experienced over the last century. It is well known that urban centers tend to be less reliant on tradition and are more progressive as a whole. As India’s cities boomed in population, the job market grew to keep pace. Prosperity and stability were now more easily attained by an individual, and the anxiety to marry quickly and effectively was reduced as traditional marriage was viewed as a means to attain these principles. Thus, younger, more progressive generations of urban Indians are less likely than ever to participate in the antiquated system of arranged endogamy.
</p><p>India has also experimented with Affirmative Action, locally known as “reservation groups”. Quota system jobs, as well as placements in publicly funded colleges, hold spots for the 8% of India’s minority, and underprivileged groups. As a result, in states such as Tamil Nadu or those in the north-east, where underprivileged populations predominate, over 80% of government jobs are set aside in quotas. In education, colleges lower the marks necessary for the Dalits to enter.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_(62).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_%2862%29.jpg/220px-Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_%2862%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_%2862%29.jpg/330px-Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_%2862%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_%2862%29.jpg/440px-Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_%2862%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1118" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seventy-two_Specimens_of_Castes_in_India_(62).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the manuscript <i>Seventy-two Specimens of Castes in India</i>, which consists of 72 full-color hand-painted images of men and women of various religions, occupations and ethnic groups found in <a href="/wiki/Madurai" title="Madurai">Madura, India</a> in 1837, which confirms the popular perception and nature of caste as Jati, before the British made it applicable only to Hindus grouped under the <i><a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">varna</a></i> categories from the 1901 census onwards.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Caste_in_the_rest_of_South_Asia">Caste in the rest of South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Caste in the rest of South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nepal">Nepal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Nepal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_Nepal" title="Caste system in Nepal">Caste system in Nepal</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepalese</a> caste system resembles that of the Indian <i>jāti</i> system with numerous <i>jāti</i> divisions with a <i>varna</i> system superimposed for a rough equivalence. But since the culture and the society is different some of the things are different. Inscriptions attest the beginnings of a caste system during the <a href="/wiki/Licchavi_(kingdom)" title="Licchavi (kingdom)">Licchavi</a> period.  Jayasthiti Malla (1382–1395) categorized Newars into 64 castes (Gellner 2001). A similar exercise was made during the reign of Mahindra Malla (1506–1575). The Hindu social code was later set up in Gorkha by Ram Shah (1603–1636).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_among_South_Asian_Muslims" title="Caste system among South Asian Muslims">Caste system among South Asian Muslims</a></div>
<p>McKim Marriott claims a social stratification that is hierarchical, closed, endogamous and hereditary is widely prevalent, particularly in western parts of Pakistan. Frederik Barth in his review of this system of social stratification in Pakistan suggested that these are castes.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sri_Lanka">Sri Lanka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sri Lanka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_Sri_Lanka" title="Caste system in Sri Lanka">Caste system in Sri Lanka</a></div>
<p>The caste system in Sri Lanka is a division of society into strata,<sup id="cite_ref-John_Rogers_51–77_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Rogers_51–77-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> influenced by the textbook <i>varnas</i> and <i>jāti</i> system found in India. Ancient Sri Lankan texts such as the Pujavaliya, Sadharmaratnavaliya and Yogaratnakaraya and inscriptional evidence show that the above hierarchy prevailed throughout the feudal period. The repetition of the same caste hierarchy even as recently as the 18th century, in the British/Kandyan period Kadayimpoth – Boundary books as well, indicates the continuation of the tradition right up to the end of Sri Lanka's monarchy.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Caste-like_stratification_outside_South_Asia">Caste-like stratification outside South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Caste-like stratification outside South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg/170px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg/255px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg/340px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg 2x" data-file-width="569" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_soedra_een_man_uit_de_laagste_kaste_van_Bali._TMnr_60002169.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Sudra caste man from <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>. Photo from 1870, courtesy of <a href="/wiki/Tropenmuseum" title="Tropenmuseum">Tropenmuseum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Balinese_caste_system" title="Balinese caste system">Balinese caste system</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Balinese</a> caste structure has been described in early 20th-century European literature to be based on three categories – triwangsa (thrice born) or the nobility, <i>dwijāti</i> (twice born) in contrast to <i>ekajāti</i> (once born) the low folks. Four statuses were identified in these sociological studies, spelled a bit differently from the caste categories for India:<sup id="cite_ref-boon_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boon-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahminas</a> – <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priest</a></li>
<li>Satrias – <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knighthood</a></li>
<li>Wesias – commerce</li>
<li>Sudras – servitude</li></ul>
<p>The Brahmana caste was further subdivided by these Dutch ethnographers into two: Siwa and Buda. The Siwa caste was subdivided into five: Kemenuh, Keniten, Mas, Manuba and Petapan. This classification was to accommodate the observed marriage between higher-caste Brahmana men with lower-caste women. The other castes were similarly further sub-classified by these 19th-century and early-20th-century ethnographers based on numerous criteria ranging from profession, endogamy or exogamy or polygamy, and a host of other factors in a manner similar to <i>castas</i> in Spanish colonies such as Mexico, and caste system studies in British colonies such as India.<sup id="cite_ref-boon_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boon-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Naturales_3.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Naturales_3.png/220px-Naturales_3.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="306" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Naturales_3.png/330px-Naturales_3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Naturales_3.png/440px-Naturales_3.png 2x" data-file-width="522" data-file-height="725" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Naturales_3.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_people" title="Tagalog people">Tagalog</a> royal couple (<i><a href="/wiki/Maginoo" title="Maginoo">maginoo</a></i>), from the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Codex" title="Boxer Codex">Boxer Codex</a> (c. 1590)</div></div></div>
<p>In the Philippines, pre-colonial societies do not have a single social structure. The class structures can be roughly categorized into four types:<sup id="cite_ref-Scott1979_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott1979-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Classless societies - egalitarian societies with no class structure. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Mangyan_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Mangyan people">Mangyan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kalanguya_people" title="Kalanguya people">Kalanguya peoples</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott1979_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott1979-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Warrior societies - societies where a distinct warrior class exists, and whose membership depends on martial prowess. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Mandaya_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandaya people">Mandaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bagobo_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Bagobo people">Bagobo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tagakaulo_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tagakaulo people">Tagakaulo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/B%27laan_people" class="mw-redirect" title="B&#39;laan people">B'laan peoples</a> who had warriors called the <i>bagani</i> or <i>magani</i>. Similarly, in the <a href="/wiki/Cordillera_Central_(Luzon)" title="Cordillera Central (Luzon)">Cordillera highlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isneg_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Isneg people">Isneg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalinga people">Kalinga peoples</a> refer to their warriors as <i>mengal</i> or <i>maingal</i>. This society is typical for <a href="/wiki/Head-hunting" class="mw-redirect" title="Head-hunting">head-hunting</a> ethnic groups or ethnic groups which had seasonal raids (<i><a href="/wiki/Mangayaw" class="mw-redirect" title="Mangayaw">mangayaw</a></i>) into enemy territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott1979_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott1979-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Petty <a href="/wiki/Plutocracy" title="Plutocracy">plutocracies</a> - societies which have a wealthy class based on property and the hosting of periodic prestige feasts. In some groups, it was an actual caste whose members had specialized leadership roles, married only within the same caste, and wore specialized clothing. These include the <i>kadangyan</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Ifugao_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Ifugao people">Ifugao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bontoc_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Bontoc people">Bontoc</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kankanaey_people" title="Kankanaey people">Kankanaey</a> peoples, as well as the <i>baknang</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Ibaloi_people" title="Ibaloi people">Ibaloi people</a>. In others, though wealth may give one prestige and leadership qualifications, it was not a caste per se.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott1979_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott1979-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Principalities - societies with an actual ruling class and caste systems determined by birthright. Most of these societies are either <a href="/wiki/Greater_India" title="Greater India">Indianized</a> or <a href="/wiki/Islamized" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamized">Islamized</a> to a degree. They include the larger coastal ethnic groups like the <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_people" title="Tagalog people">Tagalog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_people" title="Kapampangan people">Kapampangan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visayan_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Visayan people">Visayan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moro_people" title="Moro people">Moro</a> societies. Most of them were usually divided into four to five caste systems with different names under different ethnic groups that roughly correspond to each other. The system was more or less <a href="/wiki/Feudalistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudalistic">feudalistic</a>, with the <i>datu</i> ultimately having control of all the lands of the community. The land is subdivided among the enfranchised classes, the <i>sakop</i> or <i>sa-op</i> (<a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassals</a>, lit. "those under the power of another"). The castes were hereditary, though they were not rigid. They were more accurately a reflection of the interpersonal political relationships, a person is always the follower of another. People can move up the caste system by marriage, by wealth, or by doing something extraordinary; and conversely they can be demoted, usually as criminal punishment or as a result of debt. Shamans are the exception, as they are either volunteers, chosen by the ranking shamans, or born into the role by innate propensity for it. They are enumerated below from the highest rank to the lowest:<sup id="cite_ref-Scott1979_37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott1979-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-arcilla_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arcilla-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scott2_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scott2-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>Royalty - (<a href="/wiki/Visayan_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Visayan people">Visayan</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Kadatoan" class="mw-redirect" title="Kadatoan">kadatoan</a></i>) the <i><a href="/wiki/Datu" title="Datu">datu</a></i> and immediate descendants. They are often further categorized according to purity of lineage. The power of the <i>datu</i> is dependent on the willingness of their followers to render him respect and obedience. Most roles of the datu were judicial and military. In case of an unfit <i>datu</i>, support may be withdrawn by his followers. <i>Datu</i> were almost always male, though in some ethnic groups like the <a href="/wiki/Banwaon_people" title="Banwaon people">Banwaon people</a>, the female shaman (<i><a href="/wiki/Babaylan" title="Babaylan">babaiyon</a></i>) co-rules as the female counterpart of the <i>datu</i>.</li>
<li>Nobility - (Visayan: <i><a href="/wiki/Tumao" class="mw-redirect" title="Tumao">tumao</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_people" title="Tagalog people">Tagalog</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Maginoo" title="Maginoo">maginoo</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_people" title="Kapampangan people">Kapampangan</a> <i>ginu</i>; <a href="/wiki/Tausug_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tausug people">Tausug</a>: <i>bangsa mataas</i>) the ruling class, either inclusive of or exclusive of the royal family. Most are descendants of the royal line or gained their status through wealth or bravery in battle. They owned lands and subjects, from whom they collected taxes.</li>
<li>Shamans - (Visayan: <i><a href="/wiki/Babaylan" title="Babaylan">babaylan</a></i>; Tagalog: <i><a href="/wiki/Katalonan" title="Katalonan">katalonan</a></i>) the spirit mediums, usually female or feminized men. While they weren't technically a caste, they commanded the same respect and status as nobility.</li>
<li>Warriors - (Visayan: <i><a href="/wiki/Timawa" title="Timawa">timawa</a></i>; Tagalog: <i><a href="/wiki/Maharlika" title="Maharlika">maharlika</a></i>) the martial class. They could own land and subjects like the higher ranks, but were required to fight for the <i>datu</i> in times of war. In some Filipino ethnic groups, they were often tattooed extensively to record feats in battle and as protection against harm. They were sometimes further subdivided into different classes, depending on their relationship with the <i>datu</i>. They traditionally went on seasonal raids on enemy settlements.</li>
<li>Commoners and slaves - (Visayan, <a href="/wiki/Maguindanao_people" title="Maguindanao people">Maguindanao</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Ulipon" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulipon">ulipon</a></i>; Tagalog: <i><a href="/wiki/Alipin" title="Alipin">alipin</a></i>; Tausug: <i>kiapangdilihan</i>; <a href="/wiki/Maranao_people" title="Maranao people">Maranao</a>: <i>kakatamokan</i>) - the lowest class composed of the rest of the community who were not part of the enfranchised classes. They were further subdivided into the commoner class who had their own houses, the servants who lived in the houses of others, and the slaves who were usually captives from raids, criminals, or debtors. Most members of this class were equivalent to the European <a href="/wiki/Serf" class="mw-redirect" title="Serf">serf</a> class, who paid taxes and can be conscripted to communal tasks, but were more or less free to do as they please.</li></ul></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China_and_Mongolia">China and Mongolia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: China and Mongolia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the period of <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a>, ruler <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> enforced a <i>Four Class System</i>, which was a legal caste system. The order of four classes of people was maintained by the information of the descending order were:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongolian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Semu" title="Semu">Semu</a> people</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a> people (in the northern areas of China)</li>
<li>Southerners (people of the former Southern <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>)</li></ul>
<p>Today, the <a href="/wiki/Hukou_system" title="Hukou system">Hukou system</a> is considered by various sources as the current caste system of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is also significant controversy over the <a href="/wiki/Social_classes_of_Tibet" class="mw-redirect" title="Social classes of Tibet">social classes of Tibet</a>, especially with regards to the <a href="/wiki/Serfdom_in_Tibet_controversy" title="Serfdom in Tibet controversy">serfdom in Tibet controversy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edo_society" title="Edo society">Edo society</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edo_social_structure.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Edo_social_structure.svg/220px-Edo_social_structure.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Edo_social_structure.svg/330px-Edo_social_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Edo_social_structure.svg/440px-Edo_social_structure.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="842" data-file-height="595" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edo_social_structure.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Social classes during the Edo period (<a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>In Japan's history, social strata based on inherited position rather than personal merit, were rigid and highly formalized in a system called <i>mibunsei</i> (身分制). At the top were the Emperor and Court nobles (<a href="/wiki/Kuge" title="Kuge">kuge</a>), together with the Shōgun and daimyō. Below them, the population was divided into <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">four classes</a>: <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a>, peasants, craftsmen and merchants. Only samurai were allowed to bear arms. A samurai had a right to kill any peasants, craftsman or merchant who he felt were disrespectful. Merchants were the lowest caste because they did not produce any products. The castes were further sub-divided; for example, peasants were labelled as <i>furiuri</i>, <i>tanagari</i>, <i>mizunomi-byakusho</i> among others. As in Europe, the castes and sub-classes were of the same race, religion and culture.
</p><p>Howell, in his review of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> society notes that if a Western power had colonized Japan in the 19th century, they would have discovered and imposed a rigid four-caste hierarchy in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>De Vos and Wagatsuma observe that Japanese society had a systematic and extensive caste system. They discuss how alleged caste impurity and alleged racial inferiority, concepts often assumed to be different, are superficial terms, and are due to identical inner psychological processes, which expressed themselves in Japan and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-gdvhw_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gdvhw-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Endogamy was common because marriage across caste lines was socially unacceptable.<sup id="cite_ref-gdvhw_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gdvhw-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> had its own untouchable caste, shunned and ostracized, historically referred to by the insulting term <i>Eta</i>, now called <i><a href="/wiki/Burakumin" title="Burakumin">Burakumin</a></i>. While modern law has officially abolished the class hierarchy, there are reports of discrimination against the <a href="/wiki/Buraku" class="mw-redirect" title="Buraku">Buraku</a> or <a href="/wiki/Burakumin" title="Burakumin">Burakumin</a> underclasses.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The Burakumin are regarded as "ostracised".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The burakumin are one of the main <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Japan" title="Demographics of Japan">minority groups in Japan</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hokkaid%C5%8D" class="mw-redirect" title="Hokkaidō">Hokkaidō</a> and those of <a href="/wiki/Zainichi_Korean" class="mw-redirect" title="Zainichi Korean">residents of Korean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_in_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese in Japan">Chinese</a> descent.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="wikitable floatright" style="text-align:left; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
<caption style="font-size:120%; background: #b0c4de;"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_caste_system&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Korean caste system (page does not exist)">Korean caste system</a>
</caption>

</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">Yangban</a></td>
<td>양반</td>

<td>aristocrats
</td></tr>

</td></tr>

<td>&#160;&#8226;&#32; <a href="/wiki/Baekjeong" title="Baekjeong">Baekjeong</a></td>

<td>白丁</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Untouchability" title="Untouchability">untouchables</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>

<td>노비</td>
<td>奴婢</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slaves</a> or <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfs</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;" colspan="4"><a href="/wiki/Template:Korean_caste_system" title="Template:Korean caste system"><sup>V</sup></a><sup>・</sup><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Korean_caste_system" title="Template talk:Korean caste system"><sup>T</sup></a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_(LOC).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_%28LOC%29.jpg/220px-Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_%28LOC%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_%28LOC%29.jpg/330px-Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_%28LOC%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_%28LOC%29.jpg/440px-Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_%28LOC%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1374" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Korea-History-Goban_Game_in_Seoul_Korea_1904_(LOC).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical Yangban family scene from 1904.  The Yoon family had an enduring presence in Korean politics from the 1800s until the 1970s.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Baekjeong" title="Baekjeong">baekjeong</a> (백정) were an "untouchable" outcaste of Korea. The meaning today is that of butcher. It originates in the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo-Khitan_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Goryeo-Khitan Wars">Khitan invasion of Korea</a> in the 11th century. The defeated <a href="/wiki/Khitans" class="mw-redirect" title="Khitans">Khitans</a> who surrendered were settled in isolated communities throughout Goryeo to forestall rebellion.  They were valued for their skills in hunting, herding, butchering, and making of leather, common skill sets among nomads.  Over time, their ethnic origin was forgotten, and they formed the bottom layer of Korean society.
</p><p>In 1392, with the foundation of the Confucian <a href="/wiki/Joseon_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseon dynasty">Joseon dynasty</a>, Korea systemised its own native class system. At the top were the two official classes, the <a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">Yangban</a>, which literally means "two classes". It was composed of scholars (<i>munban</i>) and warriors (<i>muban</i>). Scholars had a significant social advantage over the warriors. Below were the <i>jung-in</i> (중인-中人: literally "middle people". This was a small class of specialized professions such as medicine, accounting, translators, regional bureaucrats, etc. Below that were the <i>sangmin</i> (상민-常民: literally 'commoner'), farmers working their own fields. Korea also had a <a href="/wiki/Serf" class="mw-redirect" title="Serf">serf</a> population known as the <i>nobi</i>. The nobi population could fluctuate up to about one third of the population, but on average the nobi made up about 10% of the total population.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In 1801, the vast majority of government nobi were emancipated,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and by 1858 the nobi population stood at about 1.5% of the total population of Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-nobi_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobi-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The hereditary nobi system was officially abolished around 1886–87 and the rest of the nobi system was abolished with the <a href="/wiki/Gabo_Reform" title="Gabo Reform">Gabo Reform</a> of 1894,<sup id="cite_ref-nobi_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobi-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> but traces remained until 1930.
</p><p>The opening of Korea to foreign Christian missionary activity in the late 19th century saw some improvement in the status of the <i>baekjeong</i>. However, everyone was not equal under the Christian congregation, and even so protests erupted when missionaries tried to integrate <i>baekjeong</i> into worship, with non-<i>baekjeong</i> finding this attempt insensitive to traditional notions of hierarchical advantage.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Around the same time, the <i>baekjeong</i> began to resist open social discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> They focused on social and economic injustices affecting them, hoping to create an <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarian</a> Korean society. Their efforts included attacking social discrimination by upper class, authorities, and "commoners", and the use of degrading language against children in public schools.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/Gabo_reform" class="mw-redirect" title="Gabo reform">Gabo reform</a> of 1896, the class system of Korea was officially abolished. Following the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_Party" title="Enlightenment Party">Gabo government</a>, the new cabinet, which became the Gwangmu government after the establishment of the Korean Empire, introduced systematic measures for abolishing the traditional class system. One measure was the new household registration system, reflecting the goals of formal <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">social equality</a>, which was implemented by the loyalists' cabinet. Whereas the old registration system signified household members according to their hierarchical social status, the new system called for an occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-proper_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-proper-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While most Koreans by then had surnames and even <a href="/wiki/Bongwan" class="mw-redirect" title="Bongwan">bongwan</a>, although still substantial number of <a href="/wiki/Cheonmin" title="Cheonmin">cheonmin</a>, mostly consisted of <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a> and slaves, and <a href="/wiki/Untouchability" title="Untouchability">untouchables</a> did not. According to the new system, they were then required to fill in the blanks for surname in order to be registered as constituting separate households. Instead of creating their own family name, some cheonmins appropriated their masters' surname, while others simply took the most common surname and its bongwan in the local area. Along with this example, activists within and outside the Korean government had based their visions of a new relationship between the government and people through the concept of citizenship, employing the term <i>inmin</i> ("people") and later, <i>kungmin</i> ("citizen").<sup id="cite_ref-proper_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-proper-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: North Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Songbun" title="Songbun">Songbun</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_Human_Rights_in_North_Korea" title="Committee for Human Rights in North Korea">Committee for Human Rights in North Korea</a> reported that "Every North Korean citizen is assigned a heredity-based class and socio-political rank over which the individual exercises no control but which determines all aspects of his or her life."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Regarded as <i>Songbun</i>, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Demick" title="Barbara Demick">Barbara Demick</a> describes this "class structure" as an updating of the hereditary "caste system", combining Confucianism and Stalinism.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> She claims that a bad family background is called "tainted blood", and that by law this "tainted blood" lasts for three generations.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tibet">Tibet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tibet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Social_classes_of_Tibet" class="mw-redirect" title="Social classes of Tibet">Social classes of Tibet</a></div>
<p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heidi_Fjeld&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Heidi Fjeld (page does not exist)">Heidi Fjeld</a><sup class="noprint" style="font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://no.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heidi_Fjeld" class="extiw" title="no:Heidi Fjeld">no</a>&#93;</sup> has put forth the argument that pre-1950s Tibetan society was functionally a caste system, in contrast to previous scholars who defined the Tibetan social class system as similar to European feudal serfdom, as well as non-scholarly western accounts which seek to romanticize a supposedly 'egalitarian' ancient Tibetan society.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Asia">West Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: West Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Yazidi" class="mw-redirect" title="Yazidi">Yazidi</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Yezidi" class="mw-redirect" title="Yezidi">Yezidi</a> society is hierarchical. The secular leader is a hereditary <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">emir</a> or prince, whereas a chief <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikh</a> heads the religious hierarchy. The Yazidi are strictly <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">endogamous</a>; members of the three Yazidi castes, the <a href="/wiki/Murid" title="Murid">murids</a>, sheikhs and <a href="/wiki/Islam_Pir" class="mw-redirect" title="Islam Pir">pirs</a>, marry only within their group.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Sassanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a> society was immensely complex, with separate systems of social organization governing numerous different groups within the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicolle,_p._11_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolle,_p._11-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Historians believe society comprised four<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social classes</a>:
</p>
<ol><li>priests (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl">Asravan</span>&#8206;)</li>
<li>warriors (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl">Arteshtaran</span>&#8206;)</li>
<li>secretaries (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl">Dabiran</span>&#8206;)</li>
<li>commoners (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl">Vastryoshan</span>&#8206;)</li></ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yemen">Yemen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Yemen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Al-Akhdam" title="Al-Akhdam">Al-Akhdam</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> there exists a hereditary caste, the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a>-descended <a href="/wiki/Al-Akhdam" title="Al-Akhdam">Al-Akhdam</a> who are kept as perennial manual workers. Estimates put their number at over 3.5 million residents who are discriminated, out of a total Yemeni population of around 22 million.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Caste system in Africa">Caste system in Africa</a></div>
<p>Various sociologists have reported caste systems in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Obinna1_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obinna1-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-James_B._Watson_356–379_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James_B._Watson_356–379-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tamari1_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tamari1-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The specifics of the caste systems have varied in ethnically and culturally diverse Africa, however the following features are common – it has been a closed system of social stratification, the social status is inherited, the castes are hierarchical, certain castes are shunned while others are merely endogamous and exclusionary.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In some cases, concepts of purity and impurity by birth have been prevalent in Africa. In other cases, such as the <i>Nupe</i> of Nigeria, the <i>Beni Amer</i> of East Africa, and the <i>Tira</i> of Sudan, the exclusionary principle has been driven by evolving social factors.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Africa">West Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: West Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GriotF%C3%AAte.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/GriotF%C3%AAte.jpg/170px-GriotF%C3%AAte.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/GriotF%C3%AAte.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GriotF%C3%AAte.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Griot" title="Griot">Griot</a>, who have been described as an endogamous caste of West Africa who specialize in oral story telling and culture preservation. They have been also referred to as the bard caste.</div></div></div>
<p>Among the Igbo of <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> – especially <a href="/wiki/Enugu_State" title="Enugu State">Enugu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anambra_State" title="Anambra State">Anambra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imo_State" title="Imo State">Imo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abia_State" title="Abia State">Abia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ebonyi_State" title="Ebonyi State">Ebonyi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edo_State" title="Edo State">Edo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delta_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Delta state">Delta</a> states of the country – Obinna finds Osu caste system has been and continues to be a major social issue. The Osu caste is determined by one's birth into a particular family irrespective of the religion practised by the individual. Once born into Osu caste, this Nigerian person is an outcast, shunned and ostracized, with limited opportunities or acceptance, regardless of his or her ability or merit. Obinna discusses how this caste system-related identity and power is deployed within government, Church and indigenous communities.<sup id="cite_ref-Obinna1_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obinna1-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>osu</i> class systems of eastern <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> are derived from indigenous religious beliefs and discriminate against the "Osus" people as "owned by deities" and outcasts.
</p><p>The Songhai economy was based on a caste system. The most common were metalworkers, fishermen, and carpenters. Lower caste participants consisted of mostly non-farm working immigrants, who at times were provided special privileges and held high positions in society. At the top were noblemen and direct descendants of the original Songhai people, followed by freemen and traders.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a review of social stratification systems in Africa, Richter reports that the term caste has been used by French and American scholars to many groups of West African artisans. These groups have been described as inferior, deprived of all political power, have a specific occupation, are hereditary and sometimes despised by others. Richter illustrates caste system in <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>, with six sub-caste categories. Unlike other parts of the world, mobility is sometimes possible within sub-castes, but not across caste lines. Farmers and artisans have been, claims Richter, distinct castes. Certain sub-castes are shunned more than others. For example, exogamy is rare for women born into families of woodcarvers.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Mand%C3%A9_peoples" title="Mandé peoples">Mandé</a> societies in <a href="/wiki/Gambia" class="mw-redirect" title="Gambia">Gambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> have social stratification systems that divide society by ethnic ties. The Mande class system regards the <i>jonow</i> slaves as inferior. Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Wolof_people" title="Wolof people">Wolof</a> in Senegal is divided into three main groups, the <i>geer</i> (freeborn/nobles), <i>jaam</i> (slaves and slave descendants) and the underclass <i>neeno</i>. In various parts of West Africa, <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fulani</a> societies also have class divisions. Other castes include <i>Griots</i>, <i>Forgerons</i>, and <i>Cordonniers</i>.
</p><p>Tamari has described endogamous castes of over fifteen West African peoples, including the Tukulor, Songhay, Dogon, Senufo, Minianka, Moors, Manding, Soninke, Wolof, Serer, Fulani, and Tuareg. Castes appeared among the <i>Malinke</i> people no later than 14th century, and was present among the <i>Wolof</i> and <i>Soninke</i>, as well as some <i>Songhay</i> and <i>Fulani</i> populations, no later than 16th century. Tamari claims that wars, such as the <i>Sosso-Malinke</i> war described in the <i>Sunjata</i> epic, led to the formation of blacksmith and bard castes among the people that ultimately became the Mali empire.
</p><p>As West Africa evolved over time, sub-castes emerged that acquired secondary specializations or changed occupations. Endogamy was prevalent within a caste or among a limited number of castes, yet castes did not form demographic isolates according to Tamari. Social status according to caste was inherited by off-springs automatically; but this inheritance was paternal. That is, children of higher caste men and lower caste or slave concubines would have the caste status of the father.<sup id="cite_ref-tamari1_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tamari1-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Africa">Central Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Central Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2019</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Ethel M. Albert in 1960 claimed that the societies in <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a> were caste-like social stratification systems.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in 1961, Maquet notes that the society in <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> can be best described as castes.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a>, noted Maquet, considered themselves as superior, with the more numerous <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> and the least numerous <a href="/wiki/Twa" title="Twa">Twa</a> regarded, by birth, as respectively, second and third in the hierarchy of Rwandese society. These groups were largely endogamous, exclusionary and with limited mobility.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Horn_of_Africa">Horn of Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Horn of Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:170px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NSRW_Africa_Midgan.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/NSRW_Africa_Midgan.png" decoding="async" width="168" height="199" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="168" data-file-height="199" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NSRW_Africa_Midgan.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Madhiban" title="Madhiban">Madhiban</a> (Midgan) specialize in leather occupation. Along with the Tumal and Yibir, they are collectively known as <i>sab</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In a review published in 1977, Todd reports that numerous scholars report a system of social stratification in different parts of Africa that resembles some or all aspects of caste system. Examples of such caste systems, he claims, are to be found in Ethiopia in communities such as the <a href="/wiki/Gurage_people" title="Gurage people">Gurage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Konso_people" title="Konso people">Konso</a>. He then presents the Dime of Southwestern Ethiopia, amongst whom there operates a system which Todd claims can be unequivocally labelled as caste system. The Dime have seven castes whose size varies considerably. Each broad caste level is a hierarchical order that is based on notions of purity, non-purity and impurity. It uses the concepts of defilement to limit contacts between caste categories and to preserve the purity of the upper castes. These caste categories have been exclusionary, endogamous and the social identity inherited.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alula_Pankhurst" title="Alula Pankhurst">Alula Pankhurst</a> has published a study of caste groups in SW Ethiopia.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kafa" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Kafa">Kafa</a>, there were also traditionally groups labeled as castes. "Based on research done before the Derg regime, these studies generally presume the existence of a social hierarchy similar to the caste system. At the top of this hierarchy were the Kafa, followed by occupational groups including blacksmiths (Qemmo), weavers (Shammano), bards (Shatto), potters, and tanners (Manno). In this hierarchy, the Manjo were commonly referred to as hunters, given the lowest status equal only to slaves."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Borana_Oromo" class="mw-redirect" title="Borana Oromo">Borana Oromo</a> of southern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> also have a class system, wherein the Wata, an acculturated hunter-gatherer group, represent the lowest class. Though the Wata today speak the <a href="/wiki/Oromo_language" title="Oromo language">Oromo language</a>, they have traditions of having previously spoken another language before adopting Oromo.<sup id="cite_ref-Westermann_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Westermann-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditionally nomadic <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali people</a> are divided into clans, wherein the <a href="/wiki/Rahanweyn" title="Rahanweyn">Rahanweyn</a> agro-pastoral clans and the occupational clans such as the <a href="/wiki/Madhiban" title="Madhiban">Madhiban</a> were traditionally sometimes treated as outcasts.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> As Gabboye, the Madhiban along with the <a href="/wiki/Yibir" title="Yibir">Yibir</a> and Tumaal (collectively referred to as <i>sab</i>) have since obtained political representation within <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, and their general social status has improved with the expansion of urban centers.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France_and_Spain">France and Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: France and Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2019</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>For centuries, through the modern times, the majority regarded <a href="/wiki/Cagot" title="Cagot">Cagots</a> who lived primarily in the Basque region of France and Spain as an inferior caste, the untouchables. While they had the same skin color and religion as the majority, in the churches they had to use segregated doors, drink from segregated fonts, and receive communion on the end of long wooden spoons. It was a closed social system. The socially isolated Cagots were endogamous, and chances of social mobility non-existent.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hansson1996_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansson1996-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In July 2013, the UK government announced its intention to amend the <a href="/wiki/Equality_Act_2010" title="Equality Act 2010">Equality Act 2010</a> to "introduce legislation on caste, including any necessary exceptions to the caste provisions, within the framework of domestic discrimination law".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Section 9(5) of the Equality Act 2010 provides that "a <a href="/wiki/Minister_(government)" title="Minister (government)">Minister</a> may by <a href="/wiki/Statutory_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Statutory order">order</a> amend the statutory definition of race to include caste and may provide for exceptions in the Act to apply or not to apply to caste".
</p><p>From September 2013 to February 2014, <a href="/wiki/Meena_Dhanda" title="Meena Dhanda">Meena Dhanda</a> led a project on "Caste in Britain" for the UK <a href="/wiki/Equality_and_Human_Rights_Commission" title="Equality and Human Rights Commission">Equality and Human Rights Commission</a> (EHRC).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In view of <a href="/wiki/W._Lloyd_Warner" title="W. Lloyd Warner">W. Lloyd Warner</a>, the relationship between Blacks and Whites in the US historically showed many features of caste like residential segregation, marriage restrictions.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Discrimination based upon socio-economic factors are historically prevalent within the country.
</p><p>According to Gerald D. Berreman, in the two systems, there are rigid rules of avoidance and certain types of contacts are defined as contaminating. In India, there are complex religious features which make up the system, whereas in the United States race and color are the basis of differentiation. The caste system in India and the United States has higher groups which desire to retain their position for themselves and thus perpetuate the system.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The process of creating a homogenized society by social engineering in both India and the US has created other institutions that have made class distinctions among groups evident. Anthropologist James C. Scott elaborates on how “global capitalism is perhaps the most powerful force for homogenization, whereas the state may in some instances be the defender of local difference and variety.”<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The caste system further emphasizes differences between the socio-economic classes that arise as a product of capitalism,  which makes social mobility more difficult. Parts of the United States are sometimes divided by race and class status despite the national narrative of integration.
</p><p>As a result of increased immigration to the United States, many <a href="/wiki/Indian_Americans" title="Indian Americans">Indian Americans</a> have brought traditional caste values to the United States. A survey commissioned by Equality Labs finds that caste discrimination is also playing out in the United States. 2/3 of the members of the lowest caste, called Dalits, said that they have faced workplace discrimination due to their caste. 41% have experienced discrimination in education because the caste system is now being practiced in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most of the Kakatiya records proudly describe them as Shudra.[Talbot, Austin Cynthia (2001), Pre-colonial India in Practice: Society, Region, and Identity in Medieval Andhra, Oxford University Press, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19803-123-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19803-123-9">978-0-19803-123-9</a>] Examples include the Bothpur and Vaddamanu inscriptions of Ganapati's general Malyala Gunda senani. The Kakatiyas also maintained marital relations with other Shudra families, such as the Kotas and the Natavadi chiefs. All these evidences indicate that the Kakatiyas were of Shudra origin.[Sastry, P. V. Parabhrama (1978). N. Ramesan, ed. The Kākatiyas of Warangal. Hyderabad: Government of Andhra Pradesh. OCLC 252341228 page 29]</span>
</li>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> (<cite class="citation"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.oed.com/view/Entry/28546">"caste, n."</a>, <i>Oxford English Dictionary, Second edition; online version June 2012</i>, Oxford, UK: Oxford University Press, 1989<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">5 August</span> 2012</span></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=caste%2C+n.&amp;rft.btitle=Oxford+English+Dictionary%2C+Second+edition%3B+online+version+June+2012&amp;rft.place=Oxford%2C+UK&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1989&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.oed.com%2Fview%2FEntry%2F28546&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaste" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/>) Quote: <b>caste, n.</b> 2a. spec. One of the several hereditary classes into which society in India has from time immemorial been divided; ... <i>This is now the leading sense, which influences all others.</i></li>
